<p><i><b>Tangled</b></i> is a 2010 American <a href="/wiki/3D_film" title="3D film">3D</a> <a href="/wiki/Computer_animation" title="Computer animation">computer-animated</a> <a href="/wiki/Musical_film" title="Musical film">musical</a> <a href="/wiki/Fantasy_film" title="Fantasy film">fantasy</a>-<a href="/wiki/Comedy_film" title="Comedy film">comedy film</a> produced by <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Animation_Studios" title="Walt Disney Animation Studios">Walt Disney Animation Studios</a> and released by <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Pictures" title="Walt Disney Pictures">Walt Disney Pictures</a>. Loosely based on the <a href="/wiki/Grimms%27_Fairy_Tales" title="Grimms' Fairy Tales">German fairy tale</a> "<a href="/wiki/Rapunzel" title="Rapunzel">Rapunzel</a>" in the collection of folk tales published by the <a href="/wiki/Brothers_Grimm" title="Brothers Grimm">Brothers Grimm</a>, it is the 50th <a href="/wiki/List_of_Walt_Disney_Animation_Studios_films" title="List of Walt Disney Animation Studios films">Disney animated feature film</a>. Featuring the voices of <a href="/wiki/Mandy_Moore" title="Mandy Moore">Mandy Moore</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zachary_Levi" title="Zachary Levi">Zachary Levi</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> the film tells the story of a lost, young princess with long magical hair who yearns to leave her secluded tower. Against her mother's wishes, she accepts the aid of a handsome intruder to take her out into the world which she has never seen.</p>
<p>Before the film's release, its title was changed from <i>Rapunzel</i> to <i>Tangled</i>, reportedly to market the film as gender-neutral.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LATimes_2-1"><a href="#cite_note-LATimes-2">[2]</a></sup> <i>Tangled</i> spent six years in production at a cost that has been estimated at $260 million, which if accurate, would make it the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Most_expensive_animated_films" title="Most expensive animated films">most expensive animated film ever made</a> and the fifth <a href="/wiki/List_of_most_expensive_films" title="List of most expensive films">most-expensive film</a> of all time. The film employed a unique artistic style by blending together features of <a href="/wiki/Computer-generated_imagery" title="Computer-generated imagery">computer-generated imagery</a> (CGI) and <a href="/wiki/Traditional_animation" title="Traditional animation">traditional animation</a> while using <a href="/wiki/Non-photorealistic_rendering" title="Non-photorealistic rendering">non-photorealistic rendering</a> to create the impression of a painting. Composer <a href="/wiki/Alan_Menken" title="Alan Menken">Alan Menken</a>, who had worked on prior Disney animated features, returned to score <i>Tangled</i>.</p>
<p><i>Tangled</i> premiered at the <a href="/wiki/El_Capitan_Theatre" title="El Capitan Theatre">El Capitan Theatre</a> on November 14, 2010, and went into general release on November 24. The film was well received by critics and audiences alike. The film earned $591 million in worldwide box office revenue,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mojo_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-mojo-3">[3]</a></sup> $200 million of which was earned in the United States and Canada. The film was nominated for a number of awards, including <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Original_Song" title="Academy Award for Best Original Song">Best Original Song</a> at the <a href="/wiki/83rd_Academy_Awards" title="83rd Academy Awards">83rd Academy Awards</a>. The film was released on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blu-ray_Disc" title="Blu-ray Disc">Blu-ray</a> and DVD on March 29, 2011; a short film, <i><a href="/wiki/Tangled_Ever_After" title="Tangled Ever After">Tangled Ever After</a></i>, was released later in 2012. A <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tangled:_Before_Ever_After" title="Tangled: Before Ever After">television series</a> will premiere in 2017.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Plot">Plot</span></h2>
<p>Long ago, a drop of sunlight became a golden flower capable of healing illness, decay, and injury. For hundreds of years, the flower is used by Mother Gothel to retain her youth until it is taken by soldiers of a nearby kingdom, Corona, to heal their ailing queen. Shortly afterwards, the Queen gives birth to Princess Rapunzel. While attempting to recover the flower, Gothel discovers that Rapunzel's hair has the flower's healing properties. Realizing that cutting a piece from Rapunzel's hair destroys its power, Gothel takes the baby and raises her as her own daughter in an isolated tower. Once a year, the King and Queen release <a href="/wiki/Sky_lantern" title="Sky lantern">sky lanterns</a> on Rapunzel's birthday, hoping for their daughter's return.</p>
<p>On the eve of her 18th birthday, Rapunzel asks Gothel if she may leave the tower and discover the source of the lanterns, but Gothel refuses, explaining that the outside world is a dangerous place. Meanwhile, a thief known as Flynn Rider steals Rapunzel's crown from the kingdom and inadvertently discovers the tower after ditching his allies, the Stabbington brothers. Rapunzel captures Flynn and discovers the crown, but is unaware of its significance. Rapunzel plans to show Flynn to Gothel to prove she can take care of herself, but Gothel becomes angry. Rapunzel then asks for a special paint that will take Gothel three days' round trip to obtain and return, and Gothel accepts. Rapunzel convinces a reluctant Flynn to escort her to see the lanterns in exchange for the crown.</p>
<p>Flynn takes Rapunzel to the Snuggly Duckling, a <a href="/wiki/Pub" title="Pub">pub</a> filled with the frightening people Gothel warned Rapunzel about, but who instead are charmed by Rapunzel's innocence. Royal soldiers (led by Maximus, one of the royal army's lead horses) arrive in search of Flynn. Rapunzel and Flynn eventually escape but are then trapped in a flooding cave. Resigned to his fate, Flynn reveals his real name is Eugene Fitzherbert. Rapunzel starts to reveal that her hair glows when she sings – but then realizes that this is their key to escape, as her hair provides enough light to find a way out of the cave. Eugene and Rapunzel take refuge in a forest where Gothel, now in league with the Stabbingtons, gives the crown to Rapunzel and suggests using it to challenge Eugene's interest in her.</p>
<p>Maximus finds the pair and tries to capture Eugene, but Rapunzel arranges a truce in honor of her 18th birthday. The group reaches the kingdom and enjoys the festivities, culminating in an evening cruise as the lanterns are released. There, Rapunzel gives Eugene the crown. When he sees the Stabbingtons on the shore, Eugene leaves Rapunzel and intends to let them have the crown. Instead, the brothers tie Eugene onto a boat and confront Rapunzel, saying that Eugene is escaping with the crown. Gothel sets a rescue by betraying the brothers and returns Rapunzel to the tower as Eugene and the Stabbingtons are captured.</p>
<p>At the tower, Rapunzel recognizes the symbol of the kingdom, which she had subconsciously incorporated into her paintings over the years. Realizing that she is the long-lost princess, she confronts Gothel. As Eugene is sentenced to death, the Snuggly Duckling regulars help him escape prison and then he is carried back to Gothel's tower by Maximus. Eugene enters the tower by climbing Rapunzel's hair, only to find Rapunzel bound with chains and gagged with a handkerchief. Gothel attacks Eugene and attempts to escape with Rapunzel, but Rapunzel agrees to lifelong captivity if she is allowed to heal Eugene. Eugene slices off her hair, destroying its magic and a rapidly-aging Gothel falls to her death. As Eugene dies, a heartbroken Rapunzel's tear, which still contains a bit of the sun's power, lands on his cheek and restores his life. The two return to the kingdom, where Rapunzel is reunited with her parents. The kingdom breaks out in celebration, and Eugene is pardoned for his crimes. Rapunzel and Eugene eventually marry.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Voice_cast">Voice cast</span></h2>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mandy_Moore" title="Mandy Moore">Mandy Moore</a> as <a href="/wiki/Rapunzel_(Disney)" title="Rapunzel (Disney)">Rapunzel</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slashsep10_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-slashsep10-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fletchersep10_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-fletchersep10-6">[6]</a></sup>
<ul>

</ul>
</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Zachary_Levi" title="Zachary Levi">Zachary Levi</a> as <a href="/wiki/Flynn_Rider" title="Flynn Rider">Eugene "Flynn Rider" Fitzherbert</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slashsep10_5-1"><a href="#cite_note-slashsep10-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fletchersep10_6-1"><a href="#cite_note-fletchersep10-6">[6]</a></sup></li>

</ul>
<p>Non-speaking animal characters include Rapunzel's pet chameleon <a href="/wiki/Pascal_and_Maximus" title="Pascal and Maximus">Pascal, and Maximus</a>, the horse of the head of the palace guard. Other non-speaking roles include Rapunzel's parents (the King and Queen of Corona), the other Stabbington brother, and Ulf the Mime Thug.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Development">Development</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins_and_conception">Origins and conception</span></h3>
<p>Glen Keane, the film's original director, first began working on the story for what became <i>Tangled</i> about 14 years prior to its release, then directed the film's development from 2002 to 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColliderKeaneInterview_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-ColliderKeaneInterview-9">[9]</a></sup> In October 2003, the film was announced as <i>Rapunzel Unbraided</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WSJComputerAge_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-WSJComputerAge-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nusairrapunzeladapted_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-nusairrapunzeladapted-11">[11]</a></sup> as a computer animated feature scheduled for a 2007 release<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WSJComputerAge_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-WSJComputerAge-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> which Keane described as "a <i><a href="/wiki/Shrek" title="Shrek">Shrek</a></i>-like version of the film"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-christiantodaykeane_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-christiantodaykeane-13">[13]</a></sup> that revolved around an entirely different concept. Keane said of the original plot, "It was a fun, wonderful, witty version and we had a couple of great writers. But in my heart of hearts I believed there was something much more sincere and genuine to get out of the story, so we set it aside and went back to the roots of the original fairy tale."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beliefnetkeane_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-beliefnetkeane-14">[14]</a></sup> In November 2005, <i>Unbraided</i> was pushed back to a summer 2009 release in order to give Keane "more time to work on the story."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Edwin_Catmull" title="Edwin Catmull">Ed Catmull</a>, at one point <a href="/wiki/Michael_Eisner" title="Michael Eisner">Michael Eisner</a> himself had proposed using modern-day <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco" title="San Francisco">San Francisco</a> as the initial setting at the start of the film and then somehow transporting the heroine into a fairy tale world, but Keane could not make that idea work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CreativityInc271_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-CreativityInc271-16">[16]</a></sup> The film was shut down about a week before Catmull and <a href="/wiki/John_Lasseter" title="John Lasseter">John Lasseter</a> were placed in charge of the studio in January 2006, and one of their first decisions was to restart the project and ask Keane to keep going with the film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CreativityInc271_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-CreativityInc271-16">[16]</a></sup> It had originally been announced in April 2007 that <a href="/wiki/Annie_Award" title="Annie Award">Annie</a>-nominated animator and story artist Dean Wellins would be co-directing the film alongside Glen Keane.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> On October 9, 2008, it was reported that Keane and Wellins had stepped down as directors due to other commitments, and were replaced by the team of <a href="/wiki/Byron_Howard" title="Byron Howard">Byron Howard</a> and Nathan Greno, director and storyboard director, respectively, of Disney's 2008 animated feature <i><a href="/wiki/Bolt_(2008_film)" title="Bolt (2008 film)">Bolt</a></i>. Keane stayed on as an executive producer and animation supervisor, while Wellins moved on to developing other short and feature films.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-KeaneOut_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-KeaneOut-18">[18]</a></sup> After the film's release, Keane revealed that he had "stepped back" from the role of director because of a heart attack in 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColliderKeaneInterview_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-ColliderKeaneInterview-9">[9]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Writing_and_character_development">Writing and character development</span></h3>
<p>When asked about the character of Rapunzel, Mandy Moore said that Rapunzel was a relatable character and called her a "Renaissance, bohemian" woman<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huffpostoct19_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-huffpostoct19-19">[19]</a></sup> rather than a typical Disney princess:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mtvaug27_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-mtvaug27-20">[20]</a></sup> "She doesn't know she's a princess [until the end of the film]. She's just really sort of motivated to find out what else is out there beyond this crazy tower she's lived in for 18 years," and that "she's very independent, she can take care of herself, and she's definitely come up with really entertaining ways to keep herself busy.".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mtvaug27_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-mtvaug27-20">[20]</a></sup> Moore also stated that she herself had little influences on Rapunzel: "The character was developed way before I had anything to do with it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huffpostoct19_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-huffpostoct19-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to Greno, one of the most difficult problems during the development of the film's plot was how to get Rapunzel out of the tower without immediately ending the movie, in that she had thereby escaped Mother Gothel and did not have any other specific objectives to pursue.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lark_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lark-21">[21]</a></sup> At a meeting one day, animator John Ripa floated an idea which turned out to be the solution they had been looking for: the mysterious floating lanterns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lark_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-Lark-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span></h2>
<p>On September 10, 2009, it was announced that actress and singer-songwriter <a href="/wiki/Mandy_Moore" title="Mandy Moore">Mandy Moore</a> had been cast as the voice of Rapunzel, and actor <a href="/wiki/Zachary_Levi" title="Zachary Levi">Zachary Levi</a> would provide the voice of Flynn Rider.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-slashsep10_5-2"><a href="#cite_note-slashsep10-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-fletchersep10_6-2"><a href="#cite_note-fletchersep10-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup> Mandy Moore approached the project through auditioning, when she heard that a film about the story of <i>Rapunzel</i> was being made.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huffpostoct19_19-2"><a href="#cite_note-huffpostoct19-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-canov2010_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-canov2010-23">[23]</a></sup> Moore later expressed that she had dreamed to be a Disney princess since she was young and said that with the role of Rapunzel, she had fulfilled her "ultimate childhood dream".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kidzworld_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-kidzworld-24">[24]</a></sup> She described herself as a "girly fan" of Disney classics<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kidzworld_24-1"><a href="#cite_note-kidzworld-24">[24]</a></sup> like <i><a href="/wiki/The_Little_Mermaid_(1989_film)" title="The Little Mermaid (1989 film)">The Little Mermaid</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Beauty_and_the_Beast_(1991_film)" title="Beauty and the Beast (1991 film)">Beauty and the Beast</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Aladdin_(1992_Disney_film)" title="Aladdin (1992 Disney film)">Aladdin</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lion_King" title="The Lion King">The Lion King</a></i>, and that it was an honor for her to be part of this "legacy" – the lineage of such Disney icons.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-collider_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-collider-25">[25]</a></sup> Since the film was going to be a <a href="/wiki/Musical_film" title="Musical film">musical</a>, it was required that all auditionees had to read several scenes and perform a song of their choice, to ensure that the voice actors could both act and sing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eonline_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-eonline-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-girl.com.au_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-girl.com.au-27">[27]</a></sup> For this singing section, Moore chose "<a href="/wiki/Help_Me_(Joni_Mitchell_song)" title="Help Me (Joni Mitchell song)">Help Me</a>" by <a href="/wiki/Joni_Mitchell" title="Joni Mitchell">Joni Mitchell</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huffpostoct19_19-3"><a href="#cite_note-huffpostoct19-19">[19]</a></sup> a song that she herself had covered on her fourth studio album, <i><a href="/wiki/Coverage_(album)" title="Coverage (album)">Coverage</a></i> (2003).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-coverageallmusic_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-coverageallmusic-28">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moorecoveragebillboard_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-moorecoveragebillboard-29">[29]</a></sup> Moore revealed that she had to attend several audition sessions<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-collider_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-collider-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-eonline_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-eonline-26">[26]</a></sup> and described the experiences as "pretty fun" but didn't put much hope in getting the part because she believed there would be much competition for this role;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-canov2010_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-canov2010-23">[23]</a></sup> she just performed her best without any anxiety.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huffpostoct19_19-4"><a href="#cite_note-huffpostoct19-19">[19]</a></sup> When she received a callback from Disney telling that she got the part, Moore described herself as being "over the moon": "I was working in New York at the time. I was with some friends and my husband – and I screamed as soon as I found out the news."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huffpostoct19_19-5"><a href="#cite_note-huffpostoct19-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>The film reportedly cost more than $260 million to produce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LATimes_2-2"><a href="#cite_note-LATimes-2">[2]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recording">Recording</span></h3>
<p>In <i>Tangled</i>, as with most animated films, all voice actors had to record their dialogue separately from one another to avoid <a href="/wiki/Spill_(audio)" title="Spill (audio)">bleeding</a> into each other's tracks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-movieweb_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-movieweb-30">[30]</a></sup> Mandy Moore later recalled that during recording, she had never met Donna Murphy and only met Zachary Levi once when they recorded "I See the Light".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-movieweb_30-1"><a href="#cite_note-movieweb-30">[30]</a></sup> Moore thought that this was "a good exercise in employing your imagination".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-about.com_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-about.com-31">[31]</a></sup> When recording action scenes, the voice actors had to jog a little in place in order to make their voices sound realistic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-about.com_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-about.com-31">[31]</a></sup> For the songs, Moore and Levi recorded on a soundstage with a 65-piece orchestra under the supervision of composer Alan Menken. They sang live with the orchestra for several times in order to help everyone "get a vibe" and a feel for the music and the singing, then were asked to go in isolation booths to record the actual tracks.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mouseclub_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-mouseclub-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> In order to aid animators in animating the characters, the filmmakers did interviews with the voice actors and filmed their facial expressions throughout the recording sessions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-about.com_31-2"><a href="#cite_note-about.com-31">[31]</a></sup> Disney animated films are usually animated to synchronize with recorded dialogue rather than asking the vocal talent to synchronize their delivery to animation after it is rendered. Thus, Moore felt that the recording process was challenging because at that time she had no animation to look at except for a few sketches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-huffpostoct19_19-6"><a href="#cite_note-huffpostoct19-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>Due to scheduling conflicts with other projects (Moore had to travel to different places like London or New York, and Levi could only record on weekends for five hours once every six weeks), they did not necessarily record dialogue in the same order as in the final film. "[When I came in], maybe that sequence or scene had been recorded by Mandy (Moore) already, maybe it hadn't. We'd end up doing the same scene five times, depending," Levi said.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-girl.com.au_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-girl.com.au-27">[27]</a></sup> After watching the finished film, Moore was disappointed because she felt that her voice sounded "shrill", while Levi thought that his performance sounded "incredibly nasally".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-about.com_31-3"><a href="#cite_note-about.com-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Animation">Animation</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fragonard,_The_Swing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2132" data-file-width="1663" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Fragonard%2C_The_Swing.jpg/220px-Fragonard%2C_The_Swing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Fragonard%2C_The_Swing.jpg/330px-Fragonard%2C_The_Swing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Fragonard%2C_The_Swing.jpg/440px-Fragonard%2C_The_Swing.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fragonard,_The_Swing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<i><a href="/wiki/The_Swing_(painting)" title="The Swing (painting)">The Swing</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Jean-Honor%C3%A9_Fragonard" title="Jean-Honoré Fragonard">Jean-Honoré Fragonard</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The film was made using <a href="/wiki/Computer-generated_imagery" title="Computer-generated imagery">computer-generated imagery</a> (CGI), although <i>Tangled</i> was modeled on the traditional look of oil paintings on canvas. The <a href="/wiki/Rococo" title="Rococo">Rococo</a> paintings of French artist <a href="/wiki/Jean-Honor%C3%A9_Fragonard" title="Jean-Honoré Fragonard">Jean-Honoré Fragonard</a>, particularly <i><a href="/wiki/The_Swing_(painting)" title="The Swing (painting)">The Swing</a></i>, were used as references for the film's artistic style, a style described by Keane as "romantic and lush."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AWNDisneyLegacy3D_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-AWNDisneyLegacy3D-34">[34]</a></sup> To create the impression of a painting, <a href="/wiki/Non-photorealistic_rendering" title="Non-photorealistic rendering">non-photorealistic rendering</a> was used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CGMeetup_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-CGMeetup-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Rapunzel-Disney.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="361" data-file-width="276" height="288" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1c/Rapunzel-Disney.jpg/220px-Rapunzel-Disney.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/1/1c/Rapunzel-Disney.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Rapunzel-Disney.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A concept rendering of Rapunzel by Lisa Keene, demonstrating the "luscious golden hair" Keane wanted.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Glen Keane originally wanted the film to be animated using a traditional 2D animation process. However, Disney executives David Stainton and Dick Cook announced that they would only approve the film for production if it were created using the 3D computer graphics.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Senseofcinema_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Senseofcinema-36">[36]</a></sup> In response to that demand, Glen Keane held a seminar called "<i>The Best of Both Worlds</i>", where he, with 50 Disney CGI artists and traditional artists, focused on the pros and cons of each style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> After the meeting, it was decided that the film would be made in 3D CG animation, but in a way as to become an extension of the traditional 2D Disney "aesthetic", a term which referred to the naturalistic animation that conforms to the fundamental principles of animation as documented by Frank Thomas and Ollie Johnston in the book <i>The Illusion of Life: Disney Animation</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Senseofcinema_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Senseofcinema-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>Due to limitations in computer technology, especially regarding attempts to capture the complexity of a human form, many basic principles of animation used in traditionally animated movies had been absent from earlier CGI films; but technological advancements have made it easier to blend the two, combining the strengths of each style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CGMeetup_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-CGMeetup-35">[35]</a></sup> Keane stated repeatedly he was trying to make the computer "bend its knee to the artist" instead of having the computer dictate the artistic style and look of the film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WSJComputerAge_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-WSJComputerAge-10">[10]</a></sup> By making the computer become as "pliable as the pencil", Keane's vision of a "three-dimensional drawing" seemed within reach, with the artist controlling the technology. Many of the techniques and tools that were required to give the film the quality Keane demanded did not exist when the project was started, and Walt Disney Animation Studios had to create them on their own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AWNDisneyLegacy3D_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-AWNDisneyLegacy3D-34">[34]</a></sup> Keane said, "There’s no photoreal hair. I want luscious hair, and we are inventing new ways of doing that. I want to bring the warmth and intuitive feel of hand-drawn to CGI."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AWNDisneyPastPresent_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-AWNDisneyPastPresent-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>One of the main goals of the animators was to create movement that mimicked the soft fluidity of the hand-drawn art found in older Disney animated films. Keane credited Disney 3D animator Kyle Strawitz with helping to combine CGI with the traditional hand-drawn style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CGMeetup_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-CGMeetup-35">[35]</a></sup> "He took the house from <i><a href="/wiki/Snow_White_and_the_Seven_Dwarfs_(1937_film)" title="Snow White and the Seven Dwarfs (1937 film)">Snow White</a></i> and built it and painted it so it looked like a flat painting that suddenly started to move, and it had dimension and kept all of the soft, round curves of the brushstrokes of watercolor. Kyle helped us get that <a href="/wiki/Jean-Honor%C3%A9_Fragonard" title="Jean-Honoré Fragonard">Fragonard</a> look of that girl on the swing... We are using <a href="/wiki/Subsurface_scattering" title="Subsurface scattering">subsurface scattering</a> and global illumination and all of the latest techniques to pull off convincing human characters and rich environments."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AWNDisneyLegacy3D_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-AWNDisneyLegacy3D-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<p>Rather than focusing on realism, the 3D team used an aesthetic approach.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CGMeetup_35-3"><a href="#cite_note-CGMeetup-35">[35]</a></sup> Robert Newman, the film’s stereoscopic supervisor said that "We’re using depth more artistically than ever before, and we’re not as concerned with the literal transcription of depth between camera and projector as we are the interpretation of it." To do this, they used a new technique called multi-rigging, which is made up of multiple pairs of <a href="/wiki/Virtual_camera_system" title="Virtual camera system">virtual cameras</a>. Each pair is used individually on each separate element that adds depth to a scene, like background, foreground, and characters, without adjusting for the relation with the other pairs. When sandwiched together later in production, the result was something that would be visually impossible in the real world, but which created an appealing look to the film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>As a counterpart to the appealing and cute design of Rapunzel,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GirlHowardGrenoInterview_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-GirlHowardGrenoInterview-40">[40]</a></sup> the directors wanted to make Flynn Rider "the most handsome, most attractive male lead Disney has ever had."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SRDisneyHunk_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-SRDisneyHunk-41">[41]</a></sup> They held a large "Hot Man Meeting" where they gathered about 30 women from the studio and asked them what they considered attractive in a man.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TelegraphTangledDirectors_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-TelegraphTangledDirectors-42">[42]</a></sup> They brought in hundreds of images of their favourite male actors and celebrities, which were torn and pasted back again.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TelegraphTangledDirectors_42-1"><a href="#cite_note-TelegraphTangledDirectors-42">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Disney11Things_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-Disney11Things-43">[43]</a></sup> After much deliberation, his look was eventually narrowed down to one concept drawing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SRDisneyHunk_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-SRDisneyHunk-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TelegraphTangledDirectors_42-2"><a href="#cite_note-TelegraphTangledDirectors-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Technology_development">Technology development</span></h4>
<p>Existing technology continued to present difficulties: in particular, animating hair turned out to be a challenge. Senior software engineer Kelly Ward spent six years writing programs to make it move the way they wanted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> As late as January 2010, the directors were still not sure if the Rapunzel character's length of hair was going to work. These problems were finally solved in March:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> An improved version of a hair simulation program named Dynamic Wires, originally developed for <i>Bolt</i>, was eventually used. To make hair float believably in water, and to surmount other similar challenges, <a href="/wiki/Discrete_differential_geometry" title="Discrete differential geometry">discrete differential geometry</a> was used to produce the desired effects, freeing the animators from executing these specific tasks directly, which would have taken days instead of minutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup></p>

<p>The original score for the film was composed by <a href="/wiki/Alan_Menken" title="Alan Menken">Alan Menken</a> with lyrics written by <a href="/wiki/Glenn_Slater" title="Glenn Slater">Glenn Slater</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ColliderMenkenInterview_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-ColliderMenkenInterview-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>Menken said he attempted to blend medieval music with 1960s folk rock to create the new songs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hammond_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hammond-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several songs were written, but eventually cut from the final film; "When Will My Life Begin?" replaced an earlier version called "What More Could I Ever Need?". Menken reported that that opening number went through five or six different versions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>Elsewhere, Menken reported that there was originally a love song called "You Are My Forever" that Mother Gothel sang to Rapunzel in a motherly way, but was reprised later in the film by Flynn Rider in a romantic way. This idea was apparently replaced with the two songs "Mother Knows Best" and "<a href="/wiki/I_See_the_Light" title="I See the Light">I See the Light</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup></p>
<p>The song "Something That I Want" performed by Grace Potter from <a href="/wiki/Grace_Potter_and_the_Nocturnals" title="Grace Potter and the Nocturnals">Grace Potter and the Nocturnals</a> is featured in the closing credits. This version features some of the lyrics that were re-written and sung by Potter herself. The Latin American Spanish version of the song, titled "<i>Algo quiero querer</i>", was recorded by Colombian pop-singer, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fanny_L%C3%BA" title="Fanny Lú">Fanny Lú</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>The soundtrack peaked at number 44 on the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_200" title="Billboard 200"><i>Billboard</i> 200</a>, number 7 on the <a href="/wiki/Billboard_charts" title="Billboard charts">Soundtrack</a> chart, and number 3 on the <a href="/wiki/Kid_Albums" title="Kid Albums">Kid Albums</a> chart.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-billboard200_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-billboard200-54">[54]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-billboardsound_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-billboardsound-55">[55]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-billboardkids_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-billboardkids-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Track_listing">Track listing</span></h3>
<p>All tracks written by <a href="/wiki/Alan_Menken" title="Alan Menken">Alan Menken</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glenn_Slater" title="Glenn Slater">Glenn Slater</a> except track 20 which is written and composed by <a href="/wiki/Grace_Potter_and_the_Nocturnals" title="Grace Potter and the Nocturnals">Grace Potter</a>. All original scores composed by Menken.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-liner_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-liner-47">[47]</a></sup>.</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Release">Release</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Home_media">Home media</span></h3>
<p><i>Tangled</i> was released by <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Studios_Home_Entertainment" title="Walt Disney Studios Home Entertainment">Walt Disney Studios Home Entertainment</a> as a four-disc combo pack on March 29, 2011. The combo pack includes a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blu-ray_3D" title="Blu-ray 3D">Blu-ray 3D</a>, standard Blu-ray, DVD, and digital copy. A two-disc Blu-ray/DVD combo pack and single DVD are also available. Bonus features for the Blu-ray include deleted scenes, two alternate opening sequences, two extended songs, and an inside look at how the film was made. The DVD includes only the two Original Storybook Openings and the 50th Animated Feature Countdown.</p>
<p>Sales of <i>Tangled</i> in the US and Canada exceeded $95 million in DVD and Blu-ray sales, the highest grossing DVD of the year 2011; its home video sales exceeded the film's earnings in its first week in theaters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> The film sold a record 2,970,052 units (the equivalent of $44,521,079) in its first week in North America, the largest opening for a 2011 DVD. It dominated for two weeks on the DVD sales chart and sold 6,657,331 units ($102,154,692) as of July 18, 2012.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-homevideosales_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-homevideosales-61">[61]</a></sup> It has also sold 2,518,522 Blu-ray units ($59,220,275) by May 29, 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> As of January 20, 2016, the film has earned a total of $215 million in home video sales in the United States and Canada ($155 from DVD sales and $60 million from Blu-Ray sales).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-homevideosales_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-homevideosales-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">Reception</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Box_office">Box office</span></h3>
<p><i>Tangled</i> premiered in Paris on November 17, exclusively screening at the <a href="/wiki/Le_Grand_Rex" title="Le Grand Rex">Grand Rex theatre</a> two weeks in advance of its French <a href="/wiki/Wide_release" title="Wide release">wide release</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> With over 3,800 tickets sold on its opening day, it set a new record for films showing in a single theater.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> It had a worldwide opening weekend of $86.1 million,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> and reached the summit of the worldwide box office once, on its eleventh weekend (Feb 4–6, 2011), with $24.9 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> <i>Tangled</i> earned $200,821,936 in North America, and $390,973,000 in other countries, for a worldwide total of $591,794,936.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mojo_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-mojo-3">[3]</a></sup> It was the third Disney film appearing in the Top 10 films of 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> As of 2016, it was the fifth highest-grossing film worldwide produced by <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney_Animation_Studios" title="Walt Disney Animation Studios">Walt Disney Animation Studios</a>, behind <i><a href="/wiki/Frozen_(2013_film)" title="Frozen (2013 film)">Frozen</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Zootopia" title="Zootopia">Zootopia</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lion_King" title="The Lion King">The Lion King</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Big_Hero_6_(film)" title="Big Hero 6 (film)">Big Hero 6</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Tangled</i> earned $11.9 million on its opening Wednesday,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> breaking the record for the largest pre-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thanksgiving_(USA)" title="Thanksgiving (USA)">Thanksgiving</a> Wednesday opening, a record previously held by Disney·Pixar's <i><a href="/wiki/Toy_Story_2" title="Toy Story 2">Toy Story 2</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> In its first weekend of release, it earned $48.8 million (the highest opening for Walt Disney Animation Studios, surpassing <i>The Lion King</i> ($40.9 million), and later surpassed by both <i><a href="/wiki/Wreck-It_Ralph" title="Wreck-It Ralph">Wreck-It Ralph</a></i> ($49 million) and <i>Frozen</i> ($67.4 million)), placing second for the period behind <i><a href="/wiki/Harry_Potter_and_the_Deathly_Hallows_%E2%80%93_Part_1" title="Harry Potter and the Deathly Hallows – Part 1">Harry Potter and the Deathly Hallows – Part 1</a></i>, which earned $49.1 million.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thanksgiving_Weekend_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Thanksgiving_Weekend-73">[73]</a></sup> <i>Tangled</i> had the sixth highest opening weekend for a film that did not debut at #1.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Over the traditional Wednesday-Sunday Thanksgiving holiday period, it tallied $68.7 million, again finishing in second place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thanksgiving_Weekend_73-1"><a href="#cite_note-Thanksgiving_Weekend-73">[73]</a></sup> <i>Tangled</i> also marked the second largest 3-day and 5-day Thanksgiving opening after <i>Toy Story 2</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Thanksgiving_Weekend_73-2"><a href="#cite_note-Thanksgiving_Weekend-73">[73]</a></sup> During its second weekend (post-Thanksgiving), <i>Tangled</i> declined 56% to $21.6 million, although it jumped to first place at the box office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> With a final gross of $200.8 million, it is the tenth highest-grossing film of 2010,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> and the tenth 2010 film to pass the $200 million mark;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> it was the fourth slowest film to pass this mark.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup> Unadjusted for inflation, it is the sixth highest-grossing film produced by Walt Disney Animation Studios, behind <i>The Lion King</i> ($422.8 million), <i>Frozen</i> ($400.7 million), <i>Big Hero 6</i> ($221.3 million), <i><a href="/wiki/Beauty_and_the_Beast_(1991_film)" title="Beauty and the Beast (1991 film)">Beauty and the Beast</a></i> ($219 million), and <i><a href="/wiki/Aladdin_(1992_Disney_film)" title="Aladdin (1992 Disney film)">Aladdin</a></i> ($217.4 million).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></p>
<p>On its opening weekend, it earned $17.4 million in eight territories and ranked second for the weekend behind <i>Harry Potter and the Deathly Hallows – Part 1</i> ($117.3 million).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Overseas_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Overseas-80">[80]</a></sup> It reached first place at the weekend box office outside North America three times in 2011.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> It marked the seventh highest-grossing 2010 film and the third highest-grossing 2010 animated film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> In Russia and the CIS, it set an opening-weekend record among non-sequel animated films (first surpassed by <i><a href="/wiki/Rio_(2011_film)" title="Rio (2011 film)">Rio</a></i>) and among Walt Disney Animation Studios films (surpassed by <i>Frozen</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Its highest-grossing markets outside North America was Germany ($44.2 million), where it is the highest-grossing 2010 animated film,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-germany_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-germany-86">[86]</a></sup> followed by France and the Maghreb region ($39.4 million) and the UK, Ireland and Malta ($32.9 million).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Critical_reception">Critical reception</span></h3>
<p><span id="Critics"></span><span id="Critical_response"></span><span id="Critical_reaction"></span> <a href="/wiki/Rotten_Tomatoes" title="Rotten Tomatoes">Rotten Tomatoes</a> gives the film an approval rating of 89% based on 216 reviews and an average score of 7.5/10.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RottenTomatoes_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-RottenTomatoes-88">[88]</a></sup> The site's consensus is: "While far from Disney's greatest film, <i>Tangled</i> is a visually stunning, thoroughly entertaining addition to the studio's classic animated canon."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RottenTomatoes_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-RottenTomatoes-88">[88]</a></sup> Another review aggregator <a href="/wiki/Metacritic" title="Metacritic">Metacritic</a>, which assigns a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weighted_average" title="Weighted average">weighted average</a> score from 0–100 out of reviews from mainstream film critics, calculated a score of 71 based on 34 reviews.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/CinemaScore" title="CinemaScore">CinemaScore</a> polls conducted during the opening weekend, the average grade cinemagoers gave <i>Tangled</i> was an "A+" on an A+ to F scale.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/A._O._Scott" title="A. O. Scott">A. O. Scott</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_York_Times" title="The New York Times">The New York Times</a></i> positively reviewed the film as "the 50th animated feature from Disney, and its look and spirit convey a modified, updated but nonetheless sincere and unmistakable quality of old-fashioned Disneyness."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes1_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes1-91">[91]</a></sup> <i><a href="/wiki/Time_(magazine)" title="Time (magazine)">Time</a></i> film critic <a href="/wiki/Richard_Corliss" title="Richard Corliss">Richard Corliss</a> wrote that <i>Tangled</i> "wades into the <a href="/wiki/DreamWorks_Animation" title="DreamWorks Animation">DreamWorks</a> style of sitcom gags and anachronistic sass," while praising the film for achieving "the complex mix of romance, comedy, adventure and heart that defines the best Disney features."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Time1_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Time1-92">[92]</a></sup> Corliss included <i>Tangled</i> at 19 in a list of top 25 All-time Best Animated films.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kenneth_Turan" title="Kenneth Turan">Kenneth Turan</a> from <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Los_Angeles_Times" title="The Los Angeles Times">The Los Angeles Times</a></i> awarded the film four stars out of five; he described the film as a "gorgeous computer-animated look that features rich landscapes and characters that look fuller and more lifelike than they have in the past."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LATimes3_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-LATimes3-94">[94]</a></sup> Sandie Angulo Chen of <a href="/wiki/Common_Sense_Media" title="Common Sense Media">Common Sense Media</a> gave the film five out of five stars, writing, "Fantastic princess adventure is fun, with great messages."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Gael Cooper of <a href="/wiki/NBC_News" title="NBC News">NBC News</a> expressed that <i>Tangled</i> may be the best Disney film of all time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nbc1_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-nbc1-96">[96]</a></sup></p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ReelViews" title="ReelViews">James Berardinelli</a> commented on his review website <i>ReelViews</i> that the film is "entertaining and enjoyable, but not groundbreaking." He also stated Rapunzel is "not as memorable as <a href="/wiki/Snow_White_(Disney)" title="Snow White (Disney)">Snow White</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ariel_(The_Little_Mermaid)" title="Ariel (The Little Mermaid)">Ariel</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Belle_(Disney)" title="Belle (Disney)">Belle</a>" as well as stating "the songs are neither catchy nor memorable."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReelViews_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-ReelViews-97">[97]</a></sup> Todd McCarthy, film reviewer for <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hollywood_Reporter" title="The Hollywood Reporter">The Hollywood Reporter</a></i> opened his review with, "It would have been nice if Disney's self-touted 50th animated feature were one of its best, a film that could stand with the studio's classics, but the world will have to make do with <i>Tangled</i>, a passably entertaining hodgepodge of old and new animation techniques, mixed sensibilities and hedged commercial calculations."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span></h4>
<p>Music in the film received mixed reviews. Bill Graham from <a href="/wiki/Collider.com" title="Collider.com">Collider.com</a> praised them for their variations to the tempo and tone, memorable lyrics, and "blending old with new," However, he also stated that "the film’s constant mixture of tones can feel a bit off-putting for some."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collider2_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collider2-99">[99]</a></sup> Roth Cornet from <i>ScreenRant</i> was positive towards them, saying that "Alan Menken’s music is as catchy, uplifting and effecting as one would expect."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ScreenRant1_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-ScreenRant1-100">[100]</a></sup> Scott of <i>The New York Times</i> positively reviewed the music, saying that it "takes you back to a charmed world of swoony longing and sprightly mischief," with a slick and efficient atmosphere and grace notes of self-conscious classicism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nytimes1_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes1-91">[91]</a></sup> Corliss from <i>Time</i> was also positive to the songs, noting that though "don't sound on first hearing like top-drawer Menken," the songs still "smoothly fill their functions." He described the opener, "When Will My Life Begin?," as the "heroine's 'I wanna' song," a Disney tradition that stretches back to Snow White's "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Some_Day_My_Prince_Will_Come" title="Some Day My Prince Will Come">Some Day My Prince Will Come</a>." "I See the Light" was described as "a generically tuneful love ballad, which is sure to be nominated for a Best Song Oscar."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Time1_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Time1-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<p>James Berardinelli, on the other hand, negatively commented the songs as "neither catchy nor memorable."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ReelViews_97-1"><a href="#cite_note-ReelViews-97">[97]</a></sup> Tim Robey from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Daily_Telegraph" title="The Daily Telegraph">The Daily Telegraph</a></i> gave a negative review, saying that they were only "OK – there’s nothing you want to whistle on the way home."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-telegraph12_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-telegraph12-101">[101]</a></sup> Peter Bradshaw from <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guardian" title="The Guardian">The Guardian</a></i>, who gave the movie two out of five star, described the songs as "sporting a laboured selection of Broadway-style show tunes," and hence are actually added for profit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guardian_1_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian_1-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Title_change_controversy">Title change controversy</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RapunzelLogo.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="242" data-file-width="506" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/RapunzelLogo.jpg/220px-RapunzelLogo.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/RapunzelLogo.jpg/330px-RapunzelLogo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/f/f2/RapunzelLogo.jpg/440px-RapunzelLogo.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:RapunzelLogo.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Unofficial logo of <i>Rapunzel</i>, before it was changed to <i>Tangled</i>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>When first put into production, the film was promoted as having the title <i>Rapunzel Unbraided</i>, which was later changed to <i>Rapunzel</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Disney's previous animated feature <i><a href="/wiki/The_Princess_and_the_Frog" title="The Princess and the Frog">The Princess and the Frog</a></i> in 2009, while being well-received by various critics<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> and taking in nearly $270 million worldwide was not as successful as Disney had hoped. Catmull would later admit in writing that Disney Animation's faith that <i>The Princess and the Frog</i><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s excellent quality would bring in all audiences notwithstanding the word "princess" in the title was their version of "a stupid pill."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> In order to market the film to both sexes and additional age groups, Disney changed the film's name from <i>Rapunzel</i> to <i>Tangled</i> while also emphasizing Flynn Rider, the film's prominent male character, showing that his story is just as important as Rapunzel's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LATimes2_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-LATimes2-106">[106]</a></sup> Disney was criticized for altering the classic title as a <a href="/wiki/Marketing_strategy" title="Marketing strategy">marketing strategy</a>. <a href="/wiki/Floyd_Norman" title="Floyd Norman">Floyd Norman</a>, a former Disney and <a href="/wiki/Pixar" title="Pixar">Pixar</a> animator and story artist, said, "The idea of changing the title of a classic like <i>Rapunzel</i> to <i>Tangled</i> is beyond stupid. I'm convinced they'll gain nothing from this except the public seeing Disney as desperately trying to find an audience."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p>Justin Chang of <i><a href="/wiki/Variety_(magazine)" title="Variety (magazine)">Variety</a></i> compared it to changing the title of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Little_Mermaid_(1989_film)" title="The Little Mermaid (1989 film)">The Little Mermaid</a></i> to <i>Beached</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> Writing for the <a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Chronicle" title="San Francisco Chronicle">San Francisco Chronicle</a>'s blog, Margot Magowan accused Disney of sexism, writing, "Can you imagine if Disney...switched a movie title so it wouldn't risk highlighting a male star? It's awful that this kind of radical gender discrimination exists for our smallest people – little kids who come into this world with huge imaginations and aspirations, big dreams that get squashed by a bunch of billionaire guys who run massive entertainment franchises."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Magowan_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-Magowan-109">[109]</a></sup></p>
<p>On November 24, 2010, the day of the film's release, directors Nathan Greno and <a href="/wiki/Byron_Howard" title="Byron Howard">Byron Howard</a> disputed reports that the title change was a marketing decision. They said they changed the title from <i>Rapunzel</i> to <i>Tangled</i> because Rapunzel is not the only main character in the film. They went on to say that you cannot call <i><a href="/wiki/Toy_Story" title="Toy Story">Toy Story</a></i> "<i><a href="/wiki/Buzz_Lightyear" title="Buzz Lightyear">Buzz Lightyear</a></i>," and they really needed a title that represented what the film is, and that it's a duo, and it stars Rapunzel and Flynn Rider.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>In March 2014, executive producer <a href="/wiki/John_Lasseter" title="John Lasseter">John Lasseter</a> explained that Disney had changed the name to improve the film's appeal <a href="/wiki/Four-quadrant_movie" title="Four-quadrant movie">to the four quadrants</a>: "There was an audience perception that these movies were just for little girls[,] but when boys, men, whatever actually see these movies[,] they like them. So on <i>Rapunzel</i> ... we changed the name and we called it <i>Tangled</i>. We did marketing that made the people who would not normally show up say, 'Hey, this looks pretty good.'"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ChristopherWilliams_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-ChristopherWilliams-111">[111]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Accolades">Accolades</span></h3>
<p>The film has been nominated for a number of awards. <a href="/wiki/Hollywood_Foreign_Press_Association" title="Hollywood Foreign Press Association">Hollywood Foreign Press Association</a> nominated <i>Tangled</i> for two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Golden_Globe_Awards" title="Golden Globe Awards">Golden Globe Awards</a>, for <a href="/wiki/Golden_Globe_Award_for_Best_Animated_Feature_Film" title="Golden Globe Award for Best Animated Feature Film">Best Animated Feature Film</a> and <a href="/wiki/Golden_Globe_Award_for_Best_Original_Song" title="Golden Globe Award for Best Original Song">Best Original Song</a> for "I See the Light", but lost to <i>Toy Story 3</i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Burlesque_(2010_musical_film)" title="Burlesque (2010 musical film)">Burlesque</a></i> respectively.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-GoldenGlobe_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-GoldenGlobe-112">[112]</a></sup> The film also received two nominations for the <a href="/wiki/Broadcast_Film_Critics_Association" title="Broadcast Film Critics Association">Broadcast Film Critics Association</a> in the same categories, though lost to <i>Toy Story 3</i> and <i><a href="/wiki/127_Hours" title="127 Hours">127 Hours</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Critics_choice_1_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-Critics_choice_1-113">[113]</a></sup> as well as nominations for two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Annie_Awards" title="Annie Awards">Annie Awards</a>, for Best Animated Feature Film and for Writing in a Feature Production.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-annieawards_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-annieawards-114">[114]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Tangled</i> was also nominated for two Phoenix Film Critics Society Awards, Best Animated Film and Best Original Song for "I've Got a Dream," which it lost to <i>Toy Story 3</i> and <i>Burlesque</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Phoenix_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-Phoenix-115">[115]</a></sup> "I See the Light" has been nominated for <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Original_Song" title="Academy Award for Best Original Song">Best Original Song</a> at the <a href="/wiki/83rd_Academy_Awards" title="83rd Academy Awards">83rd Academy Awards</a>, but lost to "<a href="/wiki/We_Belong_Together_(Randy_Newman_song)" title="We Belong Together (Randy Newman song)">We Belong Together</a>" from <i>Toy Story 3</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-academyawards_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-academyawards-116">[116]</a></sup> It has also been nominated for 37th <a href="/wiki/Saturn_Award_for_Best_Animated_Film" title="Saturn Award for Best Animated Film">Saturn Award for Best Animated Film</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Variety37thSaturn_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-Variety37thSaturn-117">[117]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Tangled</i> won best 3D scene of the year at the second annual International 3D Society Creative Arts Awards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Tangled</i> was also nominated for favorite film in the British Academy Children Awards for Favorite Film, competing against films like <i>Harry Potter and the Deathly Hallows – Parts 1</i> &amp; <i><a href="/wiki/Harry_Potter_and_the_Deathly_Hallows_%E2%80%93_Part_2" title="Harry Potter and the Deathly Hallows – Part 2">2</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Transformers:_Dark_of_the_Moon" title="Transformers: Dark of the Moon">Transformers: Dark of the Moon</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Cars_2" title="Cars 2">Cars 2</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Kung_Fu_Panda_2" title="Kung Fu Panda 2">Kung Fu Panda 2</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BAFTABAFTA_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-BAFTABAFTA-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BAFTAfail_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-BAFTAfail-120">[120]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Franchise">Franchise</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tangled_(franchise)" title="Tangled (franchise)">Tangled (franchise)</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Video_game">Video game</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tangled:_The_Video_Game" title="Tangled: The Video Game">Tangled: The Video Game</a></div>
<p>A video game based on the film was released on November 23, 2010, for <a href="/wiki/Nintendo_DS" title="Nintendo DS">Nintendo DS</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wii" title="Wii">Wii</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Personal_computer" title="Personal computer">PC</a> platforms by <a href="/wiki/Disney_Interactive_Studios" title="Disney Interactive Studios">Disney Interactive Studios</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Short_film">Short film</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Tangled_Ever_After" title="Tangled Ever After">Tangled Ever After</a></div>
<p>Disney released a short film in 2012 titled <i>Tangled Ever After</i>. The plot revolves around Rapunzel and Eugene's wedding day.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Musical">Musical</span></h3>
<p>An abridged stage adaptation entitled <i>Tangled: The Musical</i> premiered on board the <i><a href="/wiki/Disney_Magic" title="Disney Magic">Disney Magic</a></i> of the <a href="/wiki/Disney_Cruise_Line" title="Disney Cruise Line">Disney Cruise Line</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-musical_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-musical-126">[126]</a></sup> in November 2015,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> featuring three new songs wrote by Alan Menken and Glenn Slater.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MusicalSongs_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-MusicalSongs-128">[128]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Television_series">Television series</span></h3>
<p>A television series, <i><a href="/wiki/Tangled:_The_Series" title="Tangled: The Series">Tangled: The Series</a></i>, will premiere on <a href="/wiki/Disney_Channel" title="Disney Channel">Disney Channel</a> in 2017.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TVLineMakingSeries_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-TVLineMakingSeries-129">[129]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cancelled_sequel">Cancelled sequel</span></h3>
<p>In December 2014, <i>Tangled's</i> producer, <a href="/wiki/Roy_Conli" title="Roy Conli">Roy Conli</a>, revealed that the production team had been "heavily pushed" for a feature-length sequel to the film, but when the writers and directors got together to develop one, they realized, "she [<i><a href="/wiki/Sic" title="Sic">sic</a></i>] cut her hair. It’s over!"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bodey_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bodey-130">[130]</a></sup> Conli explained that at Disney Animation under <a href="/wiki/John_Lasseter" title="John Lasseter">John Lasseter</a>, it is always the filmmakers who decide whether they are ready to make a sequel (not marketing or merchandising).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bodey_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bodey-130">[130]</a></sup> In January 2015, Conli again provided a similar explanation when pressed on this point, and also mentioned that directors Greno and Howard ultimately "weren't really interested" in following up on the story.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brew_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-Brew-131">[131]</a></sup></p>

<tr>
<th class="navbox-group" scope="row">Musicals</th>
<td class="navbox-list navbox-odd hlist" style="text-align:left;border-left-width:2px;border-left-style:solid;width:100%;padding:0px">
<div style="padding:0em 0.25em">
